@p1[9]@p2<TIE>Another explanation: It is doubled with couples@p3. [You might ask: Why did Rashi need to bring two explanations? The answer is:] These two explanations reflect two opinions in Eiruvin 53a. They are telling us not to mistakenly think that the cave itself was double—one cave within the other, or one cave on top of the other—for then it should have said @hh<HEB>מער@hbה@hh הכפולה@ee<ENG>, “the double cave.” Since @hh<HEB>מער@hbה@ee<ENG> lacks the @hh<HEB>ת@ee<ENG> of the construct state, @hh<HEB>כפולה@ee<ENG> [would consequently be an adjective and] its feminine @hh<HEB>ה@ee<ENG> would refer back to [the feminine] @hh<HEB>מער@hbה@ee<ENG>. But with @hh<HEB>מער@hbת@ee<ENG> in the construct state, @hh<HEB>המכפלה@ee<ENG> is a noun meaning “doubleness.” Thus, @hh<HEB>מערת המכפלה@ee<ENG> denotes “a cave of some other doubleness.” One opinion is that it was so named for the doubleness of the building (a house with an upper level above it) and the other opinion is that it was so named for the doubleness of the couples. And since Rashi does not decide which is closer to the plain meaning of Scripture, he cites both; they are equally good. A further answer: Rashi held that each explanation on its own raises the question: Why was the cave called @hh<HEB>מכפלה@ee<ENG>, after its doubleness, thereby omitting the main reason for its name? It should have been called after the main reason: according to one opinion, “Cave of House and Upper Level” or “Cave of Double Building”. And according to the other opinion, “Cave of Couples” or “Cave of Doubled Couples.” This is why Rashi brings both explanations—to say that it was called after the fact that it has certain things which relate to doubleness. These are: building and couples. But it was not called so for relating to only one matter. Alternatively, it was [so called] for the sake of brevity, to include two things in the word @hh<HEB>מכפלה@ee<ENG>. [Since both are equally hinted to,] both are included. We explained the name “Kiryas Arba” along similar lines. @p1[10]@p2<TIE>On that day they appointed him an officer over them<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 If it were written @hh<HEB>י@hbו@hhשב@ee<ENG> it would be in the present tense, denoting that he once lived as an equal in the midst of the sons of Cheis, and he still does. @p1[28]@p2<TIE>And a daughter confides only in her mother.@p3 Although it is written about Rachel: “She ran and told her @ITfather@it” (29:12), that was because her mother had died. (@ITBereishis Rabbah@it) @p1[29]@p2<TIE>Why did he run and for what did he run?@p3 Rashi is not asking why Lavan ran, and answering, “He said, ‘This one is rich<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>..’” Rather, Rashi is arranging the verses, as they are not written in chronological sequence. It should have said first, “When he had seen the nose ring and the bracelets on the hands of his sister, and heard the words of his sister<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.” and then, “He ran.” That is the sequence: Lavan saw the ring, heard his sister’s words, and ran. Also, it is not feasible that [the sequence follows the order of the verses]: first he ran, then he saw the ring and heard his sister’s words, and then he went out to the man. An even greater [break in sequence is that] in the middle, it says Lavan saw the ring. [On the contrary,] “He ran” and “He came” should be next to one another, not interrupted by, “When he had seen<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.” All this brought Rashi to explain, “‘He ran.’ Why did he run<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.?” Rashi is saying that the verses are written in this order so they will [state that Lavan ran, and then] explain @ITwhy@it he ran. Furthermore, there are two [possible] reasons why Lavan ran: seeing the ring, and hearing his sister’s words. When Rashi says, “And for what did he run?” he is asking: for which reason did he run? [Rashi answers:] “He saw” and “He ran” are written next to each other to show that he ran because, upon seeing the ring, he said: “He is rich.” It was not because he heard Rivkah’s words, [in which case we could] judge him favorably, that he ran to honor Eliezer or for some other reason, and not out of his lust for money. Whereas if “He saw” and “He heard” would have been written before “He ran,” [in chronological sequence,] we would not know what he ran for, or if it was for both reasons. This is why Rashi carefully quotes the wording of the verse: “Why ‘He ran’? ‘When he had seen the nose ring<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.’” From this we see that Rashi’s true intention is to arrange the verses so they will be orderly and connected. He does this by inserting a few [explanatory] words into the verses, which is his practice in most places, as known by those familiar with Rashi’s ways. Whereas if Rashi merely intended to ask and answer, as certain commentators explained, then he should [have paraphrased] and said: “Why did he run? He said, ‘This one is rich’<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>..” [According to them,] that which Rashi quotes, “When he had seen the nose ring,” before explaining, “This one is rich<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>..” is completely not understandable. @p1[37]@p2<TIE>And she will not be willing to follow you@p3. [This comment] solves three problems. Second, [Rashi’s addition of] “And she will not be willing to follow you” [solves the problem] that otherwise it would imply: as soon as you go to my father’s house you will be absolved of the oath not to take a wife from the Canaanites, whether or not she is willing to follow you. [Third, in the phrase, “Unless you @ITfirst@it go<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.”] Rashi adds the word “first” [to solve the problem] that otherwise it would imply: if you take a wife from the Canaanites, and @ITafterwards@it go [to my family,] and she is not willing to follow you, you still will be absolved of my oath. But this is not so, as Eliezer was not allowed [to take a Canaanite wife] unless he @ITfirst@it went [to Avraham’s family] and she would not be willing to follow him. [Accordingly, the final phrase in v. 38,] “Take a wife for my son,” begins a new sentence. It is as if saying: “Rather, you should take a wife for my son from my family, as mentioned above.” @p1[39]@p2<TIE>It is written @HB<HEB>אלי@ee@BO<ENG>. @p1[19]@p2<TIE>Yaakov and Eisov who are discussed in this parshah.@p3 Meaning: wherever it is written, “@ITAnd@it these,” it is a continuation of that which preceded. [They claimed] that both sons would be righteous if they came from Avraham. [Our P@ITarshah@it is thus connected to an earlier narrative, explaining why it says, “@ITAnd@it these.”] This is the reason it was necessary to say, “Yitzchok son of @ITAvraham@it,” and support it by saying, “Avraham was the father of Yitzchok,” [implying that Yitzchok’s face resembled Avraham’s]. @p1[21]@p2<TIE>To be implored and to be placated and swayed by him.@p3 [Rashi says this] because @hh<HEB>וַיֵrָתֶר@ee<ENG> (He granted his prayer) cannot mean “increase.” Rather it means “implore,” whose meaning is close to that of “increase.” [Although @hh<HEB>וַיֵrָתֶר@ee<ENG> comes later on in the verse,] this is why Rashi explains it before @hh<HEB>לנוכח אשתו@ee<ENG>: [in order to explain the meaning of the root @hh<HEB>עתר@ee<ENG>]. [Here,] it means he implored and increased his prayers. @p1@p2And similarly: “Exorbitant are the kisses of the enemy,” meaning, they@p3 @BO@ITseem@bo@it@BO exorbitant<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>. @boRashi is answering the question: It was explained above that @hh<HEB>ונעתרות@ee<ENG> also means “increasing.” But why would an enemy increase his kisses? Thus Rashi explains that they @ITseem@it exorbitant and are burdensome. I.e., an enemy kisses only once, but it seems as if he kissed exorbitantly, and even the one kiss is burdensome. [You might ask:] Why did Rashi not mention @hh<HEB>נעתרות נשיקות שונא@ee<ENG> above, when he was explaining @hh<HEB>וַיֶrְתַר@ee<ENG> which also means increasing? The answer is: Above, we could have said that @hh<HEB>עתר@ee<ENG> has two [separate] meanings: imploring and increasing. This would be similar to @hh<HEB>וישבר למצרים@ee<ENG> (41:56), where Rashi explains that @hh<HEB>שבר@ee<ENG> can mean either selling or buying. [Accordingly, @hh<HEB>נא@hh@ee<ENG> means “now,” rather than “please.”] @p1[29]@p2<TIE>You, also<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi inserts the word @hh<HEB>גם@ee<ENG> to indicate that the Torah’s word @hh<HEB>אתה@ee<ENG> means @hh<HEB>גם אתה@ee<ENG> (“You, also, do with us likewise”). Because @ITnow@it we have seen that you are the blessed one of Hashem. question: Is not the phrase @hh<HEB>בינותינו בינינו וביניך@ee<ENG> redundant? This means, “Let that oath, which already exists between us, continue now.” Rashi adds a @hh<HEB>ה@ee<ENG> to @hh<HEB>אלה@ee<ENG>, and inserts @hh<HEB>אשר@ee<ENG>, because it is recounting what happened to Avraham. And he inserts @hh<HEB>גם@ee<ENG> because Avimelech is seeking to make the same covenant with Yitzchok that he made with Avraham. Thus he says @hh<HEB>גם עתה@ee<ENG>. It is evident that Rashi, when he says @hh<HEB>עתה@ee<ENG> (now), is explaining @hh<HEB>נא@ee<ENG>. [Accordingly, @hh<HEB>נא@ee<ENG> means “now,” rather than “please.”]<QC>
@p1[35]@p2<TIE>An expression meaning: a spirit of rebellion, as in: “You were rebellious.”@p3 For if it meant @hh<HEB>מרירות@ee<ENG> (bitterness), it would not have a @hh<HEB>ת@ee<ENG> in the construct state, since the root would be @hh<HEB>מרר@ee<ENG>. [Rashi answers:] Perforce, Hashem did not save him from this cause [of blindness] so that Yaakov could receive the blessing. But a question remains: In any case, if there was smoke, why did not Rivkah, too, become blind? Thus Rashi explains that Yitzchok’s eyes were already weakened from the @ITakeidah@it, and the smoke caused him to lose his sight altogether. (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1[3]@p2<TIE>Sharpen your knife and slaughter properly<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Meaning: there should not be a nick in the knife. @ITRe’m@it asks: Granted that Yitzchok relied on Eisov, as he held him to be a good person. @p1[7]@p2<TIE>With His consent<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Question: How could Rivkah say, “In the presence of Hashem,” something which Yitzchok never said? The answer is that Rashi had a question: If Yitzchok considered Eisov righteous, why did he warn him so much on this day about @ITneveilah@it and robbery? Rashi, [by explaining, “With His consent,”] answers that Yitzchok intended the blessings to be with Hashem’s consent — that He should grant His approval. This is why Yitzchok especially exhorted Eisov on this day. [And when Rivkah heard his exhortation, she understood that Yitzchok intended to bless Eisov “in the presence of Hashem.”] Rivkah said [to herself] about this: Since Eisov is going to bring any animal, even a stolen one [see Rashi on v. 5], he also will not obey his father regarding @ITneveilah@it. If so, Hashem surely will not grant His approval. Thus she told Yaakov (v. 9, see Rashi there): “Take for me from there,” i.e., they are not stolen ones. (@ITNachalas Yaakov@it)<QC>
@p1[9]@p2<TIE>One .<TIE>.<TIE>. his Paschal offering and one .<TIE>.<TIE>. as delicious food<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 You might ask: How does Rashi know it was Pesach? This is as it says about Moshe (Devarim 31:2): “Today I am one hundred and twenty years old,” and Rashi explains: “Today, my days became complete. On this day I was born, and on this day I will die.” Since Yitzchok said (v. 2), “I do not know the day of my death,” i.e., perhaps I will die today, it must have been Pesach. (@ITMaharshal@it)<QC>

@p1[19]@p2<TIE>I am the one who brings you, and Eisov is your first-born.@p3 Otherwise, Yaakov lied. You might object: He still lied, for Eisov had sold him the birthright. (@ITRe’m@it) @ITMaharshal@it comments on this: @ITRe’m’s@it explanation differs from Bereishis Rabboh (68:7), which says: It is written @hh<HEB>ויצא@ee<ENG> (singular), and not @hh<HEB>ויצאו@ee<ENG>. Perforce, [it speaks specifically of him] because when Yaakov left, the glory and brilliance departed—which would not be so if someone else had left. @p1@p2While a righteous person is in the city, he is its glory<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 I.e., when a tzaddik is in the city, the men and people are also righteous because they are ashamed of him [and do not sin]. We need not object that Yitzchok was still there, as Bereishis Rabboh already answered: The righteousness of one tzaddik is not like the righteousness of two tzaddikim. @p1@p2“And she left the place,” which is written concerning Naomi and Ruth.@p3 Question: Why does Rashi bring a proof from Ruth, when in Ruth, he brings a proof from here? The answer is: Without the proof from Ruth, we might think that the two leavings written here are (1) the departure of his person and (2) the departure of his money, flock and cattle which left with him. Thus, Rashi brings proof from Ruth that the departure of a tzaddik makes an impression—as there, it cannot refer to the departure of their property. For it is written there that she said (Ruth 1:21), “Hashem has brought me back empty,” indicating clearly that she had no property. And in Ruth, Rashi brings a proof from here, because we might think that it makes an impression only if no tzaddik remains behind, as was the case there. But where there are tzaddikim, how do we know that the departure of a tzaddik makes an impression? Thus Rashi proves it from here, as Yitzchok and Rivkah remained, yet Yaakov’s departure still made an impression. Thus Rashi explains that the ladder was standing in Beer Sheva, as Scripture implies, and the middle of its slope reached opposite the Beis Hamikdosh, which is Yerushalayim. And its top was in Beis Eil, as the verse also implies. It cannot be that the top of its slope is opposite the Beis Hamikdosh, for then Yaakov would be outside of the ladder. This is because Beer Sheva is in southern Yehudah, while the Beis Hamikdosh is in its north. If so, by what merit would Luz, [far away in northern Binyamin,] be called the House of God and the gate of Heaven? Neither the foot nor the top of the ladder is there! Otherwise, Yaakov would have to cut down on food in order to buy clothing, and would not have [sufficient] food to eat — yet, Hashem promised him [sufficient] food! @p1[27]@p2<TIE>Is it possible that he had sons and would send his daughter<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.?@p3 You might ask: Perhaps he had younger boys [who were too young to be sent]? The answer is: If so, he would have sent Bilhah or Zilpah, who were from a concubine, rather than Rochel who was his daughter from his true wife. If he had no sons, it is understandable to send Rochel, for then she will inherit him. [According to the laws of inheritance] daughters from a true wife come before daughters from a concubine. Consequently, she would surely guard [his flock] well. But if he had sons, she would not inherit any property, as sons come before daughters according to the laws of inheritance. Were that the case, he would not have sent Rochel. Perforce, he had no sons at all. @p1@p2Speckled and spotted among the goats and dark brown among the sheep<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 @ITRe’m@it asks: If so, speckled and spotted was the sign for goats, but not for sheep. This way I will not have any of the speckled or spotted goats, or any of the speckled, ringed or spotted sheep. Only those that will be born from now on, i.e., the speckled or spotted goats, and the dark brown sheep, will be mine. But Yaakov did not say to take away the dark brown goats because there were none to be found. When your father blessed you that “you shall live by your sword” (27:40), that is only “When you have cause to be grieved” (ibid). [I.e., “When the Israelites will transgress the Torah.”] But I kept the whole Torah! @ITMaharshal@it explains that Rashi is answering the question: Why did Yaakov say, “I have not become an officer<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.”? For this actually would allow the wicked Eisov to claim, “If the blessings were not fulfilled for you, you surely did not keep the mitzvos! Thus I should fulfill regarding you, ‘When you have cause to be grieved, you will throw off his yoke from your neck.’” Therefore Yaakov told him, “I kept all the 613 mitzvos. Nevertheless, I have not become an officer.” @p1[6]@p2<TIE>These are neither from the heavens nor from the earth.@p3 Although they are nourished from the earth, they do not actually grow from the earth. Otherwise, [if Yaakov meant as follows, a contradiction arises.] First he said, “I lived as a stranger,” to convey that the blessings were not fulfilled. Then he said @hh<HEB>ויהי לי שור וחמור@ee<ENG>, [detailing his many blessings. And he must have meant @hh<HEB>שור וחמור@ee<ENG> in plural,] as is customary to refer to many oxen as “ox.” This is obviously so, for he sent Eisov a whole camp as a gift, and would be proven a liar if he meant, “I acquired one ox [and one donkey].” According to Rashi’s other explanation above, Yaakov was saying: “Although I kept the 613 mitzvos, my father’s blessing has not been realized. I have only oxen and donkeys, not the rest of my father’s blessing, which is “from the fat of the land.” Therefore, [since my merits have not been offset by my rewards,] I do not fear you. @p1@p2To announce that I am coming to you.@p3 [Rashi knows this] because if Yaakov was sending [the messengers] to tell him, “I lived as a stranger with Lavan<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>. And that cannot be, for such a message is not a reason to find favor; it is merely [a reason] that Eisov should not hate him. Therefore Rashi explains, “To announce that I am coming to you” to be subservient to you — and through this will I find favor in your eyes. @ITMaharshal@it writes that Rashi is answering the question: Yaakov said before, “This is what you should say.” Why does he say [again], “To tell my master”? Perforce it means, “To announce that I am coming.” In this way I will find favor, because when people send [messages] to appease someone, they do not come themselves. But I will even come myself! All this is because I am at peace with you. @p1[7]@p2<TIE>But he acts towards you as the wicked Eisov.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Otherwise, why does it say, “Eisov”? Yaakov had only one brother! (@ITRe’m@it) A further explanation: Rashi is answering the question: It should say @hh<HEB>באנו אל עשו אחיך@ee<ENG>, like it is written @hh<HEB>אל עשו אחיו@ee<ENG> at the beginning of the @ITparshah@it. @p1[8]@p2<TIE>@HB<HEB>ויירא@ee@BO<ENG> lest he be killed.@p3 You might ask: Had Hashem not promised him when he left his father’s house, “And I will bring you back to this land” (28:15)? The Gemara (Berachos 4a, Sanhedrin 98b) answers: [He feared that] sin might cause [the promise not to be fulfilled]. Another answer: The promise was only, “And I will bring you back to this land,” meaning to Eretz Yisrael, but not to his father’s house. See @ITRe’m@it, @ITR. Noson, Gur Aryeh@it and @ITMinchas Yaakov@it. @p1@p2@HB<HEB>ויצר לו@BO<ENG> that he might kill others.@p3 [Question: Why did this distress him?] The answer is: Yaakov feared he might kill Eisov, and Yitzchok loved Eisov and considered him a good person. It would pain Yitzchok greatly and bring him to curse Yaakov. (@ITMidrash Tanchuma@it) An alternative answer: Yaakov surely did not fear killing Eisov, for it says in Sanhedrin 72a: “If someone comes to kill you, kill him first.” Rather, Yaakov feared that he might kill Eisov’s men, who came to kill not Yaakov but Yaakov’s men. Only Eisov came to kill Yaakov. Indeed, Eisov’s men would be attacking Yaakov’s men, and one may save the attacked even by taking the attacker’s life. But if injuring the attacker’s limb would suffice to save the one being attacked, then for killing the attacker [instead of just injuring him] one is liable for the death penalty. Thus, Yaakov feared he might kill them in the confusion of war even where injuring a limb would suffice. Some ask: Why did Yaakov fear being killed? Eisov said, “When the mourning days for my father approach, I will kill my brother Yaakov” (27:41), and Yitzchok was still alive. A further question: Why did Eisov send Elifaz to kill Yaakov [at the time that Yitzchok was alive]? It seems the answer is: When Eisov said he will not kill Yaakov while his father is alive, this applied only if Yaakov was with Yitzchok. But if he was elsewhere, Eisov would kill him. See @ITMinchas Yaakov@it. @p1[9]@p2<TIE>Against his will, because I will do battle with him.@p3 I.e., while I will do battle with him, the second camp surely will have time to escape. @ITMaharshal@it writes that Rashi inferred this because it is not written, “@ITPerhaps@it the remaining camp will survive,” but, “The remaining camp @ITwill@it survive.” This implies, “Against his will, because I will do battle<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>..” Rashi said all this, based on Scriptural inferences, to support @ITChazal’s@it teaching that Yaakov prepared himself for three things. [Rashi says “against his will,” i.e., it surely will be.] This is because the verse cannot mean “perhaps” [the remaining camp will survive]. If so, it would be more logical to gather everyone in one camp to fight Eisov—for maybe they will win due to greater numbers. However if the remaining camp will [surely] survive, against Eisov’s will, it is proper for Yaakov to have divided them into two camps. For it is better to act in a manner that will surely save one camp, even if it places the other camp in greater danger, than to put them in a situation in which they are not sure whether they all will be lost or saved. (@ITNachalas Yaakov@it) @p1@p2He prepared himself for three things.@p3 All three are derived from Scripture [see @ITPesikta D’Rav Kahana@it 19:3]. And “war” is derived from, “The remaining camp will survive,” showing that this phrase refers to war. Thus Rashi explains it as, “I will do battle with him.”<QC>
@p1[10]@p2<TIE>Return to the Land of your father and to your birthplace, and I will be with you<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Yaakov was saying: Since You promised to be with me when I come to the Land of my fathers, if I die on the way, it will turn out that Your promise to me was not fulfilled. @p1[11]@p2<TIE>My merits have diminished because of the kindnesses and truth.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 @hb<HEB>מ@hhכל החסדים@ee<ENG> means @ITbecause@it of the kindnesses. @hh<HEB>ו@hbאת@hh עבדך@ee<ENG> means, “That you have done @ITwith@it me.” Rashi is explaining that [in this verse,] @hh<HEB>את@ee<ENG> means “with.” @p1@p2Lest from the time You made me the promises, I have become tainted by sin<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 For the reason of diminished merits alone, Yaakov would not have feared, as there is no death without sin and no suffering without iniquity (Shabbos 55a). Thus Rashi brings also the reason of “tainted by sin.” And for the reason of “tainted by sin” alone, we might think that Yaakov still need not have feared, as his merits would outweigh his sins. Thus Rashi brings also the reason of diminished merits. Due to both of these reasons, Yaakov was frightened. @p1@p2“Making Your words come true.<TIE>.<TIE>.”@p3 Rashi is answering the question: Just because Hashem spoke [true promises], should Yaakov’s merits diminish? For Rashi said above, “My merits have diminished.” [Therefore Rashi explains, “You have @ITkept@it all the promises.”] And why did Rashi say, “You have @ITkept@it,” rather than, “You have @ITdone@it,” as the verse says? Because [the fulfillment of] what one says is not called @hh<HEB>עשייה@ee<ENG>, but @hh<HEB>שמירה@ee<ENG>. I.e., “keeping” it is the same as “doing” it. @p1@p2The Midrashic explanation is: He placed his staff into the Jordan [River] and the Jordan split.@p3 According to this, [Yaakov is saying that] Hashem did him two good deeds: the splitting the Jordan, and the [dividing into] two camps. But according to the simple meaning, there is only one kindness. @p1[12]@p2<TIE>“From the hand of my brother who does not act towards me as a brother<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.”@p3 Here too, Rashi deduced this because @hh<HEB>מיד@ee<ENG> is written twice. And Yaakov would not use an expression of honor [about Eisov] when speaking to Hashem. (@ITMaharshal@it)<QC>
@p1[13]@p2<TIE>@HB<HEB>היטיב@BO<ENG> on account of your merits, and @HB<HEB>איטיב@BO<ENG> on account of your fathers’ merits. @boAlthough the Torah speaks in the language of man, [thus a double verb need not be expounded,] here it is clear that it is to be expounded. @p1[14]@p2<TIE>In his possession.@p3 There is a question [on the first explanation]: Why does it need to say @hh<HEB>בידו@ee<ENG>? (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1[15]@p2<TIE>Once an animal has become pregnant it does not accept the male.@p3 Rashi is saying that it is the nature of an animal to mate as often as enjoyment calls for and free time permits. Since he-goats and rams are free all day, Yaakov gave them ten females each. This enabled them to fulfill their desire and maximize offspring for the owner. And so Yaakov gave each [type of male animal] according to its free time. This raises a question: Although he-goats and rams are free all day, they do not know that their mating with many females benefits the owner. Perhaps one male will fulfill its desire with one female, mating with it repeatedly? To this Rashi answers: “Once an animal has become pregnant.<TIE>.<TIE>.” This forces the male to separate from her and go to a second and a third, etc. @ITRe’m@it explained differently; the reader may decide. See Kesubos 62a, which says that free time serves as the determining factor. (@ITNachalas Yaakov@it) @p1[16]@p2<TIE>And their young were with them.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi is saying that there were also thirty young ones. @p1[2]@p2<TIE>In order to mislead his father.@p3 Question: Why did Eisov not call Bosmas, [too,] by a good name in order to trick his father? Thus Scripture calls her by the name of Bosmas (incense), to indicate this. See there. (@ITR. Meir Stern@it) @p1@p2Indicating that he came upon Seir’s wife.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 @ITRe’m@it asks: How does Rashi know that Timna was an illegitimate child? @p1[24]@p2<TIE>Indicating that Tzivon came upon his mother and bore Anoh.@p3 You might ask: How does Rashi know that Tzivon came upon his mother, Seir’s wife? Perhaps Seir, Tzivon’s father, came upon Tzivon’s wife and begat Anoh from her. And people thought Anoh was Tzivon’s son, but Anoh was in fact Seir’s son and Tzivon’s brother. The answer is: It is logical to attribute an act of corruption to one who is corrupt. And before we find that Tzivon was corrupt (Rashi, v. 2), as he came upon his daughterin-law, Anoh’s wife. But there is no reason to say that both [Anoh and Seir] were corrupt. You might object: And before, how did Rashi know that Tzivon was corrupt and came upon his daughter-in-law? Perhaps Anoh was the corrupt one and came upon his mother, Tzivon’s wife. The answer is: If so, there still would be two corrupt people—Anoh and Seir. And since we could rather attribute it all to Tzivon, we do so. (@ITRe’m@it) @ITMaharshal@it objects: Why should we consider two women corrupt—Anoh’s wife who was Tzivon’s daughter-in-law, and Seir’s wife who was Tzivon’s mother? [It is possible that Tzivon’s wife was the corrupt one in both incidents.] The answer is: Women are not called corrupt, because they are passive. The man, who does the act, is called corrupt. An alternative answer: Women can say they were forced to have relations. But a man cannot thereby exempt himself, since he is capable of having relations only if he is willing. @p1@p2“No person has ever consulted me about a wound caused by a white mule and lived.”@p3 I.e., R. Chanina was a doctor, and said: “No one ever consulted me about a wound caused by a white mule and I gave him a cure.”<QC>
@p1[31]@p2<TIE>Yaakov established a corresponding number in whose days Eisov’s kingdom ceased<TIE>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi means: It is written [about Yaakov and Eisov], “One government will be mightier than the other” (25:23), implying they will not be simultaneously great [see Rashi there]. Hendel@it) @p1[40]@p2<TIE>Called by the names of their states.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 I.e., when the verse says, “According to their places, by their names,” it means they are named after their places. @p1[1]@p2<TIE>After it has written for you concerning the settlements of Eisov and his descendants.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 @ITRe’m@it explains that Rashi is answering the question: Why does Scripture not write first @hh<HEB>ואלה תולדות יעקב.<TIE>.<TIE>.@ee<ENG>, and then @hh<HEB>וישב יעקב.<TIE>.<TIE>.@ee<ENG>, which is the same format that it writes about Eisov. [Thus, this section connects to the previous one as regards to settling,] as also the section about Eisov explained his settlement and that of his descendants. @p1@p2And that they are suspect in illicit relationships .<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Question: How could the sons of Yaakov have been suspect of doing these things? And if they were suspect, why then was Yoseif punished on account of this? The answer is: They surely were not suspect of doing these things; Yoseif erred. And he was punished because he should have looked further into the matter before suspecting them. He thought they ate limbs of living animals because they ate from a properly slaughtered animal that was still convulsing. But the meat was permitted since it was after @ITshechitah@it. Or, he saw them eating a @ITben pekoa@it [an animal found alive in the womb of its mother that had been properly slaughtered]. It is permitted [to eat it,] due to the @ITshechitah@it of its mother. And even if it [develops into] a big bull it does not require to be ritually slaughtered. [It only needs to be slaughtered] because it @ITappears@it to be forbidden. Yoseif however, considered it as being the limb of a living animal because it nonetheless requires to be ritually slaughtered, due to appearance. They demeaned the sons of the handmaidens by calling the sons of Bilhah and Zilpah “sons of handmaidens.” Although this was true, and even Scripture refers to them so, Yoseif thought it implied that their sons were non-Jewish slaves. But he erred, because their sons were not non-Jewish slaves, for Bilhah and Zilpah were freed women who assisted Rochel and Leah. Regarding illicit relationships: a man may not use another man’s wife to serve him (Kiddushin 70a), and Yoseif saw them using the services or doing business with a married woman, so he suspected them. But he erred, because a man is forbidden to use the services of a married for intimate purpose only, such as washing his face, hands and feet, or making his bed. But, non-intimate matters or doing business with her are permitted. You might ask: How does Rashi know that Yoseif suspected them of all three things? The answer is: Rashi himself answers this by saying, “Yoseif was, in, turn, smitten by these three.<TIE>.<TIE>.” Another answer: There is a connection between @hh<HEB>דבתם @hbרעה@ee<ENG> written here, and v. 33 @hh<HEB>חיה @hbרעה@hh אכלתהו@ee<ENG> (“A wild beast devoured him”). Just like there it involved a limb from a living creature, since a wild beast devours its prey alive, so too here it involved a limb from a living creature. [The matter regarding non-Jewish] slaves is derived from what it is written about a handmaiden in Shemos 21:8: @hh<HEB>אם @hbרעה@hh בעיני אדוניה@ee<ENG>. Just like @hh<HEB>רעה@ee<ENG> there involves slavery, so too here it involves slavery. [The matter regarding] illicit relationships is derived from what it is written in 39:9 regarding Yoseif: @hh<HEB>ואיך אעשה ה@hbרעה@hh הגדולה הזאת@ee<ENG> (“How can I do such a great evil”). Just like @hh<HEB>רעה@ee<ENG> there refers to illicit relationships, so too here it refers to illicit relationships. @p1@p2“They slaughtered a goat” when he was sold, and they did not eat it while it was alive.@p3 I.e., the Torah had no need to write that they slaughtered it, since they did not eat it. Perforce, it comes to tell us that even though they did not intend to eat it, they nonetheless slaughtered it [because they were meticulous about @ITshechitah@it]. @p1[3]@p2<TIE>Because he was born to him in his old age.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 You might ask: Was not Binyamin born in an older age than Yoseif? (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1@p2That he was sold to Potiphar, traders.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 I.e., @hh<HEB>פסים@ee<ENG> is the abbreviation for @hb<HEB>פ@hhוטיפר@ee<ENG>, @hb<HEB>ס@hhוחרים@ee<ENG>, @hb<HEB>י@hhשמעאלים@ee<ENG>, @hb<HEB>מ@hhדינים@ee<ENG>. @p1[10]@p2<TIE>After he had told it to his brothers he retold it to his father in their presence.@p3 You might ask: Why did Yoseif not tell the first dream to his father? The answer is: Yoseif knew that most dreams follow their verbal interpretation, and his brothers interpreted it for the good, so he did not want to tell it to his father. But his brothers did not interpret his second dream for him, as they realized he was telling them his dreams so they will make a good interpretation. Thus he had to tell it to his father so he will interpret it for him. His father did so, and rebuked him so that his brothers would not hate him. (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1@p2Because he was bringing hatred upon himself.@p3 I.e., Yoseif was bringing hatred upon himself. That is why Yaakov rebuked him — not because Yaakov did not want the dream to come true. For it is written in the next verse, “His father kept the matter in mind.” [See Rashi there.] @p1@p2But he did not know that these things were referring to Bilhah who had raised him like his mother.@p3 You might ask: How does Rashi know that Yaakov did not know this? Perhaps Yaakov knew, but he questioned [the dream] for the reason Rashi had given before: that Yoseif should not bring hatred onto himself. @ITRe’m@it <HS><HS>asks [the question this way]: Regarding the explanation of “These things were referring to Bilhah,” Rashi says that Yaakov did not know. But had he known, he would not have said, “Has your mother not already died?” lest his sons realize [that his comment was just a trick]. Yet, regarding the explanation of “No dream is without meaningless things,” why did Yaakov not fear that his sons, too, might know this rule and thus say that his comment was just a trick? @ITMaharshal@it answers: [In the first explanation,] had Yaakov known that these things were referring to Bilhah, his sons surely would have known too, because this is a matter grasped through reasoning. Saying, “Shall I, your mother and your brothers come.<TIE>.<TIE>.” would not make them ignore the matter. But [in the second explanation,] it then says, “His father kept the matter in mind,” because Yaakov had an oral tradition that no dream is without meaningless things. His sons however, did not know this tradition. @p1@p2However, our Sages learned from here that no dream is without meaningless things.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi is explaining why Yaakov questioned [the dream] and said, “Shall I, your mother and your brothers come.<TIE>.<TIE>.” Did he not know that no dream is without meaningless things? Perforce, [he said it] “To make his sons ignore.<TIE>.<TIE>.” @p1@p2But Yaakov intended to make his sons ignore.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 I.e., our Sages hold that “your mother” must mean Yoseif’s true mother. However, “No dream is without meaningless things.” @p1[12]@p2<TIE>There are dots on the word @HB<HEB>את@BO<ENG> indicating that they went only to “pasture” themselves.@p3 Rashi means that if there were no dots on @hh<HEB>את@ee<ENG>, it would be connected to @hh<HEB>צאן@ee<ENG>, and mean “to pasture the sheep.” But now that it has dots, it is as if the @hh<HEB>את@ee<ENG> was not written at all. Consequently, @hh<HEB>לרעות@ee<ENG> is not connected to the next word, and means: “They went to pasture.” I.e., to pasture themselves. And @hh<HEB>צאן אביהם בשכם@ee<ENG> is a separate statement. But if @hh<HEB>את@ee<ENG> was completely omitted from the verse, it would not prove the point. Surely we could still say it means “to pasture themselves,” but it would be more logical to say that the words are connected, and form one statement. Thus, although the @hh<HEB>את@ee<ENG> would be omitted, we would say it is an abbreviated verse. But now that @hh<HEB>את@ee<ENG> is written, and it has dots, it is considered as erased. This indicates that the words are not connected; they are rather two statements. Otherwise, why does it have dots?<QC>
@p1[13]@p2<TIE>A term indicating humility and readiness.@p3 You might ask: Why did Rashi not give this explanation on (22:11) @hh<HEB>אברהם אברהם ויאמר @hbהנני@ee<ENG>, and on (Shemos 3:4) @hh<HEB>משה משה ויאמר @hbהנני@ee<ENG>? So it seems to me.<QC>

@p1[17]@p2<TIE>They have removed themselves from brotherhood.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: How does the angel’s reply pertain to Yoseif’s inquiry? Yoseif only requested to be told where they were, while the angel replied, “They have traveled from here.” Furthermore, Yoseif already knew they traveled from there, since he did not find them there. I.e., they do not want there to be twelve [brothers], rather they want to kill you, and there will be only eleven. @p1@p2A person cannot accept consolation for one who is alive but is thought to be dead.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 The question is asked: Did Yaakov not know that consolation cannot be accepted for a live person thought to be dead? @p1[15]@p2<TIE>Because she was sitting at the crossroads.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: How is, “Because she had covered her face,” a reason for [Yehudah to think] that she was a harlot? On the contrary, it shows she is modest! Thus Rashi explains, “Because she was sitting at the crossroads.” Then Rashi says that he did not recognize her “because she had covered her face.” Had he recognized her he would not have “thought she was a harlot.” You might ask: How could Yehudah go to a harlot? The Patriarchs and their children kept the Torah, so how could he transgress, “There shall not be a harlot” (Devarim 23:18)? @ITRe’m@it answers: Perhaps he performed @ITkiddushin@it with her through money or a written contract, and the goat-kid [which he promised to send] was only to appease her [i.e., not a harlot’s fee]. An alternate answer: He had relations with her as an act of @ITkiddushin. @itAlthough it says in Kiddushin 12b that it is impudent to perform @ITkiddushin @itby having relations, this is only when there are witnesses. Privately it is permitted, and here there were no witnesses — only his [close] friend who was considered as himself. Or, it was not in his friend’s presence. Accordingly, the verse says @hh<HEB>הבה נא@ee<ENG> (v.16), which Rashi explains as, “Prepare yourself and your mind for this,” it means to prepare for @ITkiddushin @itby having relations. An alternate answer: Yehudah held that, “There shall not be a harlot,” applies only between a Jew and a Jewess, but a non-Jewess is merely forbidden Rabbinically. @p1@p2At her father-in-law’s house, she was a modest woman, therefore, he did not suspect her.@p3 [Rashi knows this] because otherwise he would have recognized her when they spoke and also during their having relations, since afterwards she must have uncovered her face. This is why Rashi brings the Midrash. (@ITDevek Tov@it) @p1[18]@p2<TIE>@HB<HEB>עזקתך ושושיפך@BO<ENG>, the ring that you use as a seal.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: Why does Onkelos translate @hh<HEB>חותמך@ee<ENG> as @hh<HEB>עזקתך@ee<ENG>, which means “your ring”? So too here, [@hh<HEB>פתילך@ee<ENG> means,] “The cloak with which you cover yourself.” @p1@p2Strong men, as he was; and righteous men, as he was.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: Why is it written @hh<HEB>ותהר @hbלו@ee<ENG>? @p1[23]@p2<TIE>Let her keep what she has.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: He already gave her it. This is a minority of a month, and cannot be considered a month, whereas the verse says: “About three months later,” implying three whole months. @ITRe’m@it answers: Perhaps a fetus is discernable after a third of the pregnancy only if it includes the majority of the first month, the whole second month, and the majority of the third month. @p1@p2They, therefore, sentenced her to be burned.@p3 You might ask: Why was she liable for burning? She was neither married nor betrothed, and only then is a Kohein’s daughter burned. Although she needed @ITyibum, @itthis does not make her liable for death because [promiscuity in such a case] is merely a Biblical prohibition: “The wife of the deceased shall not marry out” (Devarim 25:5). And once she had been sentenced to death, it was by burning because we find elsewhere that the execution of a Kohein’s daughter was by burning. @p1[1]@p2<TIE>Every form of the word @HB<HEB>קץ@BO<ENG> means “the end.”@p3 It is obvious that @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> means “end,” as Onkelos translates it. However, [Rashi is answering the] question: Why does Scripture write the word @hh<HEB>ימים@ee<ENG>? It would be understandable if @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> did not [always] mean “end,” but [also can mean] “some of,” as in @hh<HEB>מ@hbקצ@hhה אחיו@ee<ENG>, “From some of his brothers” (47:2). Accordingly, here it mentions @hh<HEB>ימים@ee<ENG> to indicate that it was two full years, not part of two years. But if @hh<HEB>מקץ@ee<ENG> always means “end,” why does Scripture write @hh<HEB>ימים@ee<ENG>? Rashi answers: We might think @hh<HEB>מקץ@ee<ENG> means “some of,” and it @ITalways @itmeans this, unless it is evident otherwise from the verse. That is why it is written here @hh<HEB>ימים@ee<ENG> — to show that it means “end,” and not “some of.” Consequently, @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> @ITalways@it means “end.” For it is evident here that @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> means “end,” and we make a @ITgezeirah shavah@it between @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> [written here, and any other instance of] @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG>. Thus Rashi says, “And [henceforth,] every form of the word @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> means ‘the end’.” So it seems to me, as opposed to @ITRe’m’s @itexplanation, that Rashi says every form of the word @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> means “the end” because Rashi wishes to distinguish @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> from @hh<HEB>קצה@ee<ENG>. This is because @hh<HEB>קצה@ee<ENG> sometimes means “end,” as in: “At the end (@hh<HEB>מקצה@ee<ENG>) of three years” (Devarim 14:28). And it sometimes means “side,” as in: “One cherub on this side (@hh<HEB>מקצה@ee<ENG>)” (Shemos 25:19). And it sometimes means “some of,” as in: “From some of (@hh<HEB>מקצה@ee<ENG>) his brothers” (47:2). Although [the word @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG>] appeared several times previously in Scripture, [and Rashi did not comment there,] this is not problematic, as many similar cases exist [where Rashi does not explain a word the first time it appears]. @ITRe’m@it writes: We need not object that it says, “At the @hh<HEB>קץ@ee<ENG> of seven years, you are to make @ITshemitah@it” (Devarim 15:1), which apparently means at the beginning of @ITshemitah@it, as it is written, “But in the seventh year you must let it rest” (Shemos 23:11). For [the answer is:] The verse in Devarim is speaking of releasing debts (@hh<HEB>השמטת כספים@ee<ENG>), and this is at the end of @ITshemitah@it. @p1@p2All other rivers are not referred to as @HB<HEB>יאורים@BO<ENG> except for the Nile.<TIE>.<TIE>. @boRashi is answering the question: Why is it written @hb<HEB>ה@hhיאור@ee<ENG>, with a definite article? @p1[38]@p2<TIE>“Can we find another like this one”.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 I.e., the meaning of @hb<HEB>ה@hhנמצא@ee<ENG> here is not the same as in @hb<HEB>ה@hhנהיה כדבר הגדול הזה@ee<ENG> (Devarim 4:32), where it means, “Did such a great thing ever happen in the world?!” Otherwise, Onkelos would have translated it here as @hh<HEB>האשתכח כדין@ee<ENG> (Did something like this ever exist?!) @p1@p2He is Potiphar and was called Poti Phera.@p3 Rashi deduced this from the fact that it is written, “Pharaoh gave Yoseif the name.<TIE>.<TIE>.and he gave him Osnas.<TIE>.<TIE>.” That is, Pharaoh gave Osnas to Yoseif. Now, what difference does it make that Pharaoh was the one who gave her to Yoseif? It must be that Poti Phera is Potiphar, and it makes a great difference, as follows: Pharaoh wanted to bestow greatness upon Yoseif and dress him royally. But he feared people would not allow Yoseif this greatness since he was a slave. Furthermore, the laws of Egypt stated that a slave may not rule nor wear prestigious garments. Thus Pharaoh [arranged that Potiphar] give Osnas to Yoseif as a wife. If a master gives his slave a wife, the slave is freed — and surely if he gives him his own daughter. From this we may conclude that Poti Phera is Potiphar. @p1@p2As a result of becoming castrated.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 His castration may be learned from the term @hh<HEB>פרע@ee<ENG> (torn), but not from @hh<HEB>פוטיפר@ee<ENG>. So it seems to me. @p1@p2Because he had lusted to sodomize Yoseif.@p3 You might ask: Earlier it is written, “Your slave did such things to me” (39:19), and Rashi explains there: “She told him this when they were intimate.” And this was well after he bought Yoseif. [Does this not imply that he was not castrated?] The answer is: He did not have actual relations, only an embracing and kissing. And this explains why Rashi used the phrase @hb<HEB>ענייני@hh תשמיש כאלה@ee<ENG> (@ITmatters@it of intimacy such as these), i.e., but not actual relations. Another answer: The castration affected only his testicles and not his membrum, as the @ITRadak@it explains. @p1[47]@p2<TIE>The word does not lose its meaning of doing, making.@p3 [Accordingly,] @hh<HEB>ותעש@ee<ENG> refers to @hh<HEB>הארץ@ee<ENG>, which made storehouses. And @hh<HEB>הארץ@ee<ENG> refers to the people of the land, as in: “When a land sins” (Yechezkel 14:12).<QC>

@p1[1]@p2<TIE>Why do you show yourselves.<TIE>.<TIE>. as though you are satiated?”@p3 Meaning: If you show yourselves to them as being satiated and having grain, they will come and ask us for food, since they are our relatives. Therefore, go and buy food so they will think we have no food. But in fact they did have food at the time. Accordingly, @hh<HEB>תתראו@ee<ENG> means satiation.<QC>
@p1@p2Similar to: @HB<HEB>ומרוה גם הוא יורא@BO<ENG>. The @hh<HEB>א@ee<ENG> of @hh<HEB>תתר@hbא@hhו@ee<ENG> replaces the @hh<HEB>ו@ee<ENG> of the root @hh<HEB>ר–ו–ה@ee<ENG>. There are many similar examples. The verse means: He who satisfies others with his learning in this world will himself be satisfied in the World to Come. You might ask: How is this proof for the last explanation, that @hh<HEB>תתראו@ee<ENG> is a term for leanness? On the contrary, it is a proof that it means satiation! The answer is: Rashi is bringing a proof for both explanations. For the first explanation, the plain meaning is a valid proof. And for the last explanation too it is a proof: @hh<HEB>תתראו@ee<ENG> is like @hh<HEB>תתרוו@ee<ENG>, but it means “not satiated.” This is similar to Rashi’s explanation of @hh<HEB>הולך ערירי@ee<ENG> (15:2) as “childless,” although @hh<HEB>ער@ee<ENG> means an heir, i.e., one who has sons. And it is also similar to what is written @hh<HEB>ודשנו את המזבח@ee<ENG> (Bamidbar 4:13), where Rashi explains: “They shall @ITtake away@it the ashes from the Altar,” although @hh<HEB>דשן@ee<ENG> means ashes. The same is true here. It is as if Scripture had written @hh<HEB>תתרוו@ee<ENG> since an @hh<HEB>א@ee<ENG> can be replaced by a @hh<HEB>ו@ee<ENG>, because the letters @hh<HEB>א–ה–ו–י@ee<ENG> are interchangeable. Thus @hh<HEB>מרוה@ee<ENG>, too, could mean “satiation,” yet will fit exactly with @hh<HEB>תתראו@ee<ENG>. Accordingly, the meaning of @hh<HEB>ומרוה גם הוא יורא@ee<ENG> is: He who is not satiated in this world will be satiated in the World to Come. @p1[2]@p2<TIE>This alludes to the 210 years.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 The correct text of Rashi omits, “He did not say @hh<HEB>לכו@ee<ENG> but rather @hh<HEB>רדו@ee<ENG>.” This is because no teaching can be derived from @hh<HEB>רדו@ee<ENG>. Scripture needs to write @hh<HEB>רדו@ee<ENG> (go down) — for Eretz Yisrael is higher than the other lands, so @hh<HEB>רדו@ee<ENG> is the right term for going from Eretz Yisrael to Egypt, as @ITRe’m@it writes. And so the term @hh<HEB>ירידה@ee<ENG> is written in the entire section@IT. @it Rashi is answering the question: Why does Scripture need to write, “Go down @ITthere@it”? But without the word “there,” we would be unable to expound anything. Alternatively, we could say that the correct text of Rashi includes, “He did not say @hh<HEB>לכו@ee<ENG> but rather @hh<HEB>רדו@ee<ENG>,” and Rashi is answering the question: Why is it written @hh<HEB>רדו@ee<ENG>, which connotes downfall? Perforce, “This alludes to the 210 years.<TIE>.<TIE>.”<QC>

@p1[9]@p2<TIE>Onkelos translates it: @HB<HEB>בדקא דארעא@BO<ENG> as in  @HB<HEB>בדק הבית@BO<ENG>.<TIE>.<TIE>. @boMeaning: breach in the [wall of the] town. @p1[10]@p2<TIE>Do not say this (@HB<HEB>לא תאמר כן@BO<ENG>).<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi added in the words@hh<HEB>תאמר כן @ee<ENG> (say this) because Yoseif had declared them as spies, so the proper word [to deny this] would be @hh<HEB>אַל@ee<ENG>. It is like saying @hh<HEB>אבל עבדיך@ee<ENG>. @p1[12]@p2<TIE>Why did you not all enter through one gate?@p3 Yoseif knew his brothers would come to buy food, and he told the guards of the gates to write the name and father’s name of whoever came to the city, and show it to him. Ten guards came and he found here, “Reuven son of Yaakov,” and there, “Shimon ben Yaakov,” and so in every record book. Thus he knew they entered through ten gates. You might ask: Why did Rashi not explain this before, on: “You have come to see where the land is exposed” (v. 9)? The answer is: The proof for it is here, because it is written in the next verse, “Your servants are twelve brothers.<TIE>.<TIE>. and one is no more.” How did this refute his assertion that they were spies? Perforce, Yoseif had said, “[You are spies,] for you entered through ten different gates of the city,” to which they replied, “No, rather we are all the sons of one man.<TIE>.<TIE>. and one is no more.” And it was because of the one who is no more that we scattered throughout the city in order to search for him, [as Rashi explains on v. 9]. @p1[14]@p2<TIE>The statement that I made, that you are spies.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: Does the verse not imply that the brothers, too, said they were spies? For we find Yoseif responding to them, “That is just what I said.<TIE>.<TIE>. you are spies.” Yet this cannot be true, for they said, “Your servants have never been spies.” Rashi answers: The word “that” (@hh<HEB>הוא@ee<ENG>) belongs at the end of the sentence. [Accordingly,] the verse means: “What I said to you, saying you are spies, @ITthat@it is the truth.” Some ask: What was it that led Yoseif to accuse them of being spies, causing him to say, “That is just what I said.<TIE>.<TIE>. you are spies”? Another question: How would bringing Binyamin verify that they were not spies? The answer is: First he said to them, “You are spies, for you entered through ten different gates of the city,” as Rashi explained. They replied, “We are all the sons of one man, and a man does not send all his sons together to spy, lest they be caught and all killed.” He said to them, “According to this, you surely are spies. Would a man send all his sons [to buy food] without leaving one or two to serve him? Perforce, your father sent [some of] you to spy the land, but since [each of] you was afraid to be caught and did not want to [be the one to] take the chance, your father was obliged to send all of you. Furthermore, he did this so that you could take counsel from ten, because “Salvation results from much counsel” (Mishlei 11:14). To this they replied, “Your servants are twelve brothers, and behold, the youngest one stayed with our father to serve him, while the ten of us went to search for our lost brother. None would do this as faithfully as his own brothers. We risked our lives to bring him back to his father, and entered through ten gates.” Yoseif replied, “That is just what I said to you. You are spies. You claim your brother is missing for many years, but you did not search for him until now! Surely you are lying, and you came to spy. And it is also a lie that you left one brother with your father to serve him. Therefore, you shall be tested in this manner. If your brother comes with you, it is questionable whether you are spies. But if he does not come, you definitely are spies, for the reasons I stated.” This answers all the questions. In the name of @ITMaharshal@it I found somewhat similar to this.<QC>

@p1[38]@p2<TIE>He did not accept Reuvein’s offer.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 [How does Rashi know this? The answer is:] It was not because misfortune might befall Binyamin that Yaakov prevented him from going with them, although the verse states as much. Because if so, why did Yaakov not say he feared misfortune might occur on the way, when Yehudah offered (43:9), “I will be security for him”? Perforce, the argument of misfortune was merely an excuse, as Yaakov did not want to shame Reuvein by rejecting his offer. The reason he rejected Reuvein’s offer was he feared the master of the land would imprison Binyamin, as he did to Shimon, and no one could stop it. But when Yehudah assumed responsibility when he offered to take Binyamin, Yaakov allowed it because he knew that if Yehudah was involved, he would utterly dedicate himself to rescue Binyamin.<QC>

@p1[16]@p2<TIE>@HB<HEB>טבוח@BO<ENG> is not an imperative for then it should have said @HB<HEB>וטבח@BO<ENG>.@p3 And then it would be like (Shemos 4:4): @hh<HEB>שלח ידך@ee<ENG> (Stretch out your hand), and (Shemos 9:19): @hh<HEB>שלח העז@ee<ENG> (Now send word and gather), which are imperatives. @p1@p2Which, in Aramaic, means the first meal.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 I.e., in the morning. Onkelos does not explain @hh<HEB>צהרים@ee<ENG> here as he does elsewhere because people usually eat at the fourth hour, [the middle of the morning]. Even Torah scholars, [who wait the longest,] usually eat at the beginning of the sixth hour, [an hour before noon] — as it says in Shabbos 10a. @p1[18]@p2<TIE>According to Onkelos who translates as @HB<HEB>ולאסתקפא עלנא@BO<ENG>.<TIE>.<TIE>. @boI.e., this translation is for @hh<HEB>ולהתנפל@ee<ENG>. @p1@p2@HB<HEB>ולהתגולל@BO<ENG>, which he translates as @HB<HEB>לאתרברבא@BO<TIE><ENG>.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 The word @hh<HEB>לאתרברבא@ee<ENG> denotes “lordship.” You might ask: Is this translation not contrary to the [simple] meaning of @hh<HEB>להתגולל@ee<ENG>? And why does he translate [@hh<HEB>להתנפל@ee<ENG>] as “to libel,” which is not in accordance with the plain meaning of the verse? The answer is: Onkelos started by explaining @hh<HEB>להתגולל@ee<ENG> as “lordship,” based on the verse @hh<HEB>גלת הזהב@ee<ENG>. As a result, @hh<HEB>להתנפל@ee<ENG> is understandable only if we explain it as “to libel.” Had @hh<HEB>להתגולל@ee<ENG> meant “to turn things against,” following its plain meaning, then @hh<HEB>להתנפל@ee<ENG> could mean “to come down upon.” But since @hh<HEB>להתגולל@ee<ENG> means “lordship,” @hh<HEB>להתנפל@ee<ENG> must mean “libel.” (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1[20]@p2<TIE>A “come down” for us.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi deduces this from the repetitious @hh<HEB>ירד ירדנו@ee<ENG> and not because it is written “coming down” instead of “going,” as he explained on @hh<HEB>רדו שמה@ee<ENG> (42:2), see there. Although [the repetition could be explained simply because] the Torah speaks as people do, however we expound the verse when we have the opportunity to do so. (@ITRe’m@it)<QC>

@p1[10]@p2<TIE>This, too, would be only just. Truly it should be as you say.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Rashi means as follows: “Your words are now also correct,” does not relate back to [their statement of,] “He among your servants with whom it is found, @IThe@it shall die.” This is because, “When there are ten persons and a stolen item is found in the possession of one of them,” they all deserve @ITequal@it punishment [by the king’s law], whatever the punishment may be. That is why Rashi says, “You are @ITall@it responsible.” Although they had said, “He shall die,” in their bitterness they exaggerated. [Rashi elaborated because] we might think that, “He.<TIE>.<TIE>. shall die,” is what is intended by, “Your words are now also correct.” But this is not so, for [in v. 16, when] the brothers [speak to Yoseif himself, they] do not say this, [that he shall die. Perforce, it was an exaggeration]. Rashi added, “Not according to the strict letter of the law; only he with whom it was found shall be my slave,” because it was not strictly according to the law [to punish only one of them]. @p1[15]@p2<TIE>Through knowledge, reason .<TIE>.<TIE>. that you stole the goblet.@p3 I.e., although you stole the goblet I divine with, so how could I have known that you stole it? Common sense should have told you that, “A man like me.<TIE>.<TIE>.”<QC>
@p1[18]@p2<TIE>May my words penetrate your ears.@p3 [How does Rashi know it does not mean that Yehudah literally requested to whisper in Yoseif’s ear? The answer is:] They spoke to Yoseif only through an interpreter, as they did not know he understood Hebrew. So how could Yehudah speak directly to Yoseif? Another answer: It is not a king’s honor for a lowly person to whisper in his ear. (@ITRe’m@it) @p1@p2From this you may infer that he spoke harshly to him.@p3 I.e., not gently and politely as one usually speaks to a king. Otherwise, why should Yoseif grow angry? @p1@p2You are as esteemed in my eyes as a king.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 From this it follows that “For you are equal to Pharaoh” explains why Yehudah said to him, “Please my master,” by which Yehudah pleaded and supplicated to him, although Yoseif was not a king. Thus Yehudah said [in explanation]: “For you are equal to Pharaoh,” i.e., “You are as esteemed in my eyes as a king.” However, “For you are equal to Pharaoh” does not explain Yehudah’s statement of, “And do not be angry,” which he had previously made. On the contrary, since Yoseif was as esteemed as a king, this is a reason for Yehudah @ITnot@it to speak harshly to him, [so Yoseif’s anger would be justified]. @p1@p2Its Midrashic explanation is: “Ultimately you will be stricken.<TIE>.<TIE>.”@p3 [Although the simple meaning is, “You are as esteemed in my eyes as a king”], the Midrash preferred not to say that Yehudah was speaking gently, rather that he was speaking harshly. Thus it explains, “Ultimately you will be stricken.<TIE>.<TIE>.” But there is a question on the Midrash: Why did it not say, “For you @ITwill@it be like Pharaoh”? Thus Rashi offers another explanation: “Just as Pharaoh issues decrees.<TIE>.<TIE>.” I.e., right now you are like Pharaoh. But there is a question regarding this explanation: Why did it not say @hh<HEB>אתה כפרעה@ee<ENG>? Thus, both explanations are needed. And there is a question regarding all the [above] explanations: Why did it not say @hh<HEB>כפרעה כמוך@ee<ENG>, since Yoseif was being compared to Pharaoh? Thus Rashi offers another explanation: “If you will provoke me.<TIE>.<TIE>.” Now, Pharaoh is compared to Yoseif as regards being killed. But this explanation is implausible on its own because how could Yehudah have said, “Do not be angry,” when he was threatening to kill him? That is why the above explanations are also needed. (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1@p2As the Pharaoh was stricken.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 From this it follows that “For you are equal to Pharaoh” is connected to, “Let your servant speak a word in my master’s ears.” And what is the [harsh] word that I will speak to you? “For you are equal to Pharaoh,” meaning that “ultimately you will be stricken.<TIE>.<TIE>.” @p1@p2Just as Pharaoh issues decrees without carrying them out.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Meaning: Pharaoh wrote in his register that a slave may not rule nor may he wear silk garments, yet he did not carry it out because he made you a ruler though you were a slave. And “he makes promises without fulfilling them,” i.e., if he did not carry out his decree where there is no monetary loss, surely he will not carry out a promise where there is monetary loss. You, too, do the same. You promise and do not fulfill, for is this [the meaning of, “Set your eyes on him?”] “You, too, do the same” does not refer to issuing decrees without carrying them out, for nowhere do we see Yoseif issuing a decree and not carry it out. And nowhere do we see Pharaoh making a promise and not keep it. You might ask: What did Yehudah have against Pharaoh, that he so denigrated the king? The answer is: Yehudah was saying to Yoseif, “On what do you rely? Do you place your trust in Pharaoh [to protect you from our retribution]? Pharaoh himself issues decrees but does not carry them out, as he wrote in Egypt’s laws that a slave may not be a ruler yet he did not carry it out.” (@ITMaharshal@it)<QC>
@p1@p2If you will provoke me I will kill you.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 From this it follows that “For you are equal to Pharaoh” is connected to, “Do not be angry with your servant,” for if you get angry, I will kill you and your master. @p1[19]@p2<TIE>From the start you encountered us with subterfuge.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: Why did Yehudah tell Yoseif what Yoseif had spoken to him? Did Yoseif not know what he said? Rashi answers: He repeated it in order to say, “From the start you encountered us with subterfuge.” @p1@p2Why did you need to ask all these question? Were we seeking your daughter.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 [Rashi knows this] because only concerning marriage do people ask about sons and daughters and fathers and mothers. You might ask: Why does Rashi say “your daughter” pertaining to Yoseif, and “our sister” pertaining to his brothers, and not the opposite, since the brothers were older than Yoseif? The answer is: Since Yoseif was esteemed in their eyes as a king, they said, “Were we seeking your daughter.” For a son-in-law usually honors his father-in-law. Similarly with, “Were you seeking our sister,” i.e., we would need to treat you with honor so that it will be well with our sister. But the opposite would be a disgrace to Yoseif as a king, for Yoseif would need to honor them. @p1@p2Nevertheless, “We said to my master” — we withheld nothing from you.@p3 Rashi explains this so we will not ask: If Yehudah knew that Yoseif was acting with subterfuge, why did he answer him, “We have an old father.<TIE>.<TIE>.”? Rashi answers: Yehudah was saying, “Nevertheless .<TIE>.<TIE>. [we acted respectfully and withheld nothing from you].”<QC>
@p1[20]@p2<TIE>He thought, “If I tell him that he is alive, he will say, ‘Bring him to me.’”@p3 You might ask: On the contrary, was Yehudah not contradicting himself? When they had come previously to Yoseif, they said he was still alive but had been sold, as it says, “And one is no more” (42:13). The answer is: The first time they came, they said, “And one is no more.” But the second time they came, they were saying that he had died, and this verse pertains to [what they were saying] the second time. Although it is not expressly written that Yoseif asked [now for details] about their lost brother, we can still say that he asked, as we anyway have to say [something similar to] this. It is written here that Yoseif had said to them, “Bring him down to me, that I may set my eyes on him.” But we do not find it written previously that Yoseif had said this to them. Perforce, he must have said it to them although it is not written previously. And the same is true here: the second time they came, when he asked about their father, he inquired also about their lost brother — and they replied that he had died. [They so replied] out of fear. They saw he had told them to bring Binyomin, and perhaps he would tell them to search for the lost brother and bring him. So they replied that after having searched for him, they received word that he had died. @p1@p2From that mother he has no other brother.@p3 I.e., @hh<HEB>לאמו@ee<ENG> means “of his mother,” [not “to his mother”]. This is because “to his mother” can be said only if the mother is alive. But after her death, one says that he remains “of his mother.”<QC>

@p1[29]@p2<TIE>As long as he is beside me I find comfort through him.@p3 Rashi is answering the question: Why is it written here that on account of Binyomin [Yaakov said], “You will bring my white head down to the grave in evil,” whereas in 37:35 it is written [that Yaakov said] a similar phrase on account of Yoseif? It is written there, “I will go down to the grave mourning for my son [Yoseif].” Rashi answers: Because of Yoseif, they indeed had already brought Yaakov down to the grave in mourning. But now, “As long [as Binyomin is beside me I find comfort through him].” Alternatively, Rashi is answering the question: Why would Yaakov be more sorrowful for Binyomin than for Rochel, Yoseif or Shimon? @p1@p2Of his mother and his brother.@p3 You might ask: How does Rashi know that it refers also to Rochel, when she is not mentioned in the verses? The answer is: It is written (v. 27), “Two sons were born to me by my wife [Rochel].” This conveys, “Two sons were born to me from her, and she is my wife” — i.e., she especially is my wife, [more than Leah]. This is because Yaakov lived mainly with Rochel. This verse implies that he mourned for Rochel but was comforted through Yoseif. And when Yoseif died, leaving Binyomin, he was comforted through him for both. “And if he dies, it will seem to me as if all three died the same day.” But it seems to me that [the answer is:] It is written here, “You will bring my white head down to the grave in @ITevil@it,” whereas in 42:38 it [merely] is written, “You will bring my white head down to the grave in @ITsorrow@it.” Accordingly, Yaakov was saying: until now I found comfort for the loss of his mother. But now I will no longer be comforted for her, thus I will go down in @ITevil@it to the grave. This is because @ITChazal@it say (Yevamos 62b): “He who is without a wife is without good.” Consequently, he is with evil. (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1[32]@p2<TIE>By accepting to be outcast in both worlds.@p3 You might ask: Rashi said before (43:9) that “I will have sinned to you for all time” meaning in the World to Come. @p1[4]@p2<TIE>And showed them that he was circumcised.@p3 You might ask: How does Rashi know that he said “Come near to me” because he wanted to show them the circumcision? @p1[23]@p2<TIE>Aged wine from which old people derive pleasure.@p3 @ITMaharshal@it writes: [Rashi knows this] because it is not written what was the good thing that Yoseif sent him, implying it was not something notable. So why did he send it to him? Because for Yaakov it was good. Therefore Rashi explains it was aged wine which is generally not so notable, but it was noteworthy to Yaakov because old people derive pleasure from it. @p1[24]@p2<TIE>Do not become involved in halachic discussion.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 Meaning: halachic discussion requires more delving into, and your attention will thus be diverted from travelling. As a result the road will lead you astray, i.e., you will get lost (Rashi, Taanis 10b). Yoseif warned them only against involvement and delving. But he did not warn them against reciting Torah teachings, since our Rabbis of blessed memory say (ibid): “Two who were walking on the road and there were no words of Torah between them.<TIE>.<TIE>.” @p1@p2An alternate explanation. Do not take very large steps.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 @ITMaharshal@it writes that Rashi’s first explanation follows one opinion in Taanis 10b, and Rashi’s alternate explanation follows the Baraisa there. Rashi also explains, “And arrive at your evening destination while there is still daylight.” The Baraisa itself offered these two explanations [of “large steps” and “daylight”] because it understands @hh<HEB>תרגזו בדרך@ee<ENG> to mean overly intensive walking, but it did not know whether it means taking large steps or walking a long time, i.e., until nightfall. Thus it offered both explanations: one pertaining to taking large steps, and one pertaining to arriving at their destination while there is still daylight. An overly intense person takes large steps, and the Rabbis taught (ibid), “Large steps remove one-fivehundredth of a person’s eyesight.” @p1[26]@p2<TIE>@BIThat@BO he is ruler.@p3 [Rashi knows this] because @hh<HEB>כי@ee<ENG> can mean @hh<HEB>אם@ee<ENG>, and @hh<HEB>אם@ee<ENG> can mean @hh<HEB>אשר@ee<ENG> (that). (@ITMaharshal@it) @p1@p2His heart was not swayed by their words.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 [Rashi is answering the question:] Why did Yaakov not believe that Yoseif was alive? He himself had suspected them of selling him! Rashi answers: They said Yoseif was the ruler, and it is implausible that a foreigner would become the ruler, especially in Egypt where a slave may not rule. [Yaakov reasoned:] since this [i.e., that Yoseif is a ruler] is not so, that [i.e., Yoseif is alive] is also not so. @p1[4]@p2<TIE>He promised him that he will be buried in Eretz Yisrael.@p3 Rashi deduces this because it is written, “I will bring you up,” implying to Eretz Yisrael, which is higher than all the other lands. @p1[7]@p2<TIE>Asher’s daughter, Serach and Leivi’s daughter, Yocheved.@p3 You might ask: Why does Scripture write “his daughters” in the plural form? He had only Dinah! We cannot answer that this is in accordance with R. Yehudah who said that a twin sister was born with each of Yaakov’s sons, and the sons married them [i.e., each married another’s twin]. This cannot be, for the twin sisters died before coming to Egypt, as Rashi explains on v. 26. Neither can we answer that it is in accordance with R. Nechemiah who said that the wives of Yaakov’s sons were [born of fathers from other nations and mothers who were] Canaanites, and Scripture calls them Yaakov’s “daughters” because people call their sons-in-law “son” and their daughters-in-law “daughter.” Rashi cited these two conflicting opinions on the verse, “All his sons and @ITall his daughters@it rose to console him” (37:35). Accordingly, [also here] “his daughters” will mean his daughters-in-law. But this cannot be, for Scripture is counting Yaakov’s own offspring here [as it says in v. 26]. It seems the answer is: When Scripture treats the genealogy of many people, it speaks in plural even for one person. [Examples are:] “The @ITsons@it of Don were Chushim” (v. 23), and, “The @ITsons@it of Palu were Eliav” (Bamidbar 26:8). Although [it might infer that] “his daughters” means Dinah alone, we still would prefer to include [other descendants] where possible, rather than relying on stretched explanations. Thus Rashi explains [“his granddaughters” as] Serach and Yocheved, [even though Yocheved is not expressly mentioned in this section]. And so it seems from Bava Basra 143b. We need not ask then on the contrary: [If this is true, then] on the verse, “They rose to console him” (37:35), why did R. Yehudah say they were twins, and R. Nechemiah say they were daughters-in-law? Perhaps it was only Dinah! For [the answer is:] It is different there, because it is written, “@ITAll @ithis daughters.” Alternatively, it is different in this case, because here it is treating the genealogy of many people, as mentioned above. (@ITMaharshal@it) @p11@p2<TIE>One of the tellers.<TIE>.<TIE>.@p3 [Rashi says, “One of the tellers,” to convey that] “said to Yoseif” in fact means “told Yoseif,” for news is “told,” not “said.” Yaakov’s falling sick before he died was something new to the world, since before his time there was no weakness [before death]. And how did Yaakov know this [about Ephraim]? Because Ephraim was frequently with Yaakov for study.<QC>
